SACRAMENTO, " Oct. 1.â The Demo
cratic City Convention to-night nom
inated James H. Devine for Mayor with
out a dissenting voice. This makes the
fifth candidate in the field for the office
of Mayor, the others being William Land,
Republican; L. Tozer, Municipal League;
George H. Clark, Independent, and S. E.
Alderman, Socialist.
Old politicians declare that the cam
paign, which will close early In Novem
ber, will be one of the hottest waged
here in many years. George H. Clark,
the Incumbent, has cast defiance into the
teeth of the Republican organization, and
has even placed an immense electric sign
announcing E*t candidacy on top of the
very building in which the Republicans
maintain their headquarters.
In accepting the nomination to-night
Devine said that he had no newspaper to
support him and no electric devices to
light him on his \u25a0way. He openly charged
William Land with having, while in the
office of Mayor three years ago, betrayed
the Interests of the people in the matter
of buying up city bonds of questionable
validity. Devine pronounced against the
proposed mechanical filtration plan, say
ing that It -would cost vast sums to op
erate it. He oeclared that now, while
Sacramento was on the eve of making
many cosily improvements, the taxpay
ers Bhould be watchful of the men they
put into office.

paign Is Probable.

The victim? of the mine fire were nearly
all married men and their families in most

In ar.y event the mines must" lie idle
many months. Prior to the disaster of
Monday the Extension output was 35,000

NANAIMO, B. C, Oct. 1.â Time only
adds to the horror of the Extension Col
liery disaster. In all seventeen men have
lost their lives and months will elapse be
fore their bodies are recovered. The mines
are now sealed up, but at any moment an
explosion may blow out the stoppings and
â¢wreck the workings. The remedy, danger
ous though it be, \u25a0was the only one pos
sible. The mines are high up the hill and
water would have to be pumped to a
heigrht of 1000 feet and a distance of about
a mile. This course will be adopted when
the attempt to smother the fire Is proved
ineffective.

Dayton and Seabreeze Residents Con
fined in. Their Homes for
Four Days.
ST. AUGUSTINE, Fla., Oct. l.-The
cities of Dayton and Seabreeze, on the
east coast, have been shut off from the
world for four days by high water, which
inundated a large part of the towns. Com
munlcatien with them was re-established
only to-day. Many persons were unable
to leave their homes. Floods all through
the section are heavier than for years.

More Companies Join.
In anticipation of the world-wide trade
in implements which they believe is to
be theirs, the members of the combine
have for the last two days been planning
not only to enlarge their plants, but also
to erect new ones on both the Pacific and
Atlantic coasts. This, it is thought, will
be a necessity in order to properly handle
the great bulk of the export trade. Prop
ositions for sites for new plants on tho
coast have already been submitted to tha
members.

that it was to make record time. The
various roads over which the special
traveled made it possible for Mr. Morgan
and his guests to reach the Oakland mole
in exactly four days after the 'start was
made from Gotham. .
Special Train's Fast Bun.
The car occupied by Mr. Morgan Is the

luxurious and did not compare in equip

ciflc Coast.
The train crew consisted of . eighteen
persons, including, the "porters, waiters,

entire train was vestibuled and lighted by
electricity.
The train was made up of the composite

Regular Soldiers Deserting.
â¢PHOENIX, Ariz,, Oct. l.-News has
just reached here of wholesale desertions
from Fort Grant, in eastern Arizona.
Twenty soldiers left the post to-day and
started for Mexico. These are in addition
to numerous desertions on Saturday. A
strong force of troops and Indian trailers
has gone after them, trying to overtake
them before they. reach the border.

PETALUMA, Oct. 1.â The red and white
flag o'f the State Grange .flies over Ma
sonic Temple. The thirty-fourth session
of the grange was .opened by Worthy
Master Wonhen this morning. One hun
dred and forty delegates answered the
roll, all subordinate lodges but eight be
ing represented.
The grange first convened for the pur
pose of conferring the fifth, or~ Ceres, de
gree. Four candidates were introduced.
After a recess the meeting reconvened
and initiated candidates for the fourth, or
Pomona, degree. .In the afternoon reports
of grand officers 'and masters of subordi
nate granges were received.
To-night Mayor Bralnerd turned over
the keys of the city to Master Worthen
at a public reception to the visitors. The
affair took place in Masonic Temple. J.
P. Rodgers was chairman of the evening.
Mayor Brainerd's welcome to the city
was responded to by Past Master Walter
Greer, and the greeting from the local
grange; tendered by F. A. Meyer, brought
a response from Grand Lecturer Taylor.
Master Worthen of San Jose and C. W,
Emery of Oakland also delivered short
speeches. The -programme, was inter
spersed with literary and musical selec
tions and closed with a dance.
To-morrow business sessions will oc
cupy the morning and afternoon hours. A
special feature of the afternoon will be a
drill by a woman's team from, Healds
burg Grange.
Many belated delegates arrived this
evening and the reception committee has
its hands full caring for the visitors. -.

SEATTLE. Oct. 1.â The steamer Hum
boldt arrived from Skaguay to-day with
216 passengers and more than $1,000,000 in
pold, half- of it in care of the Alaska Ex
press Company. It is the second largest
shipment of the season from the Klon
dike.
The Humboldt brings the news that Sep
tember 25, the first day on which press
dispatches reached Skaguay from the
States, was regarded as an epoch in Alas
kan history. All the telegraphic news
was strongly featured. The story is also
told of a fabulously rich quartz strike in
the Atlin district, assaying $25,000 in gold
to the ton.

VICTORIA, B. C, Oct. 1.â The Duke and

eral response made by the Duke to the
foimal welcomes. Perfect weather pre

spite. Amphion. Phaeton and Condor; the

and the, Dominion steamer Quadra. As

yards. A shore battery at Work Point
, took up the refrain of the guns, firing an

Special Dispatch to The CalL
SAN BERNARDINO, Oct. 1.â In a run
away accident at Colton, three miles from
this city, yesterday, Charles Medhurst
was instantly killed and Captain R. J.
xaartin fatally injured. Both men are
members of the Board of City Trustees or
Colton and are prominent fruit growers
of this section.
Mr. Medhurst and Captain Martlii were
returning from the former's ranch north
of the city with a load of furniture. Just
as they were nearing the Santa Fe tracks
the horses became frightened and ran
away. The" team , had not gone 200 yards
when Medhurst and Martin were thrown
out of the wagon, near the Santa Fe sta
tion. Â«Â£he wheels of the heavy wagon
crossetrthe small of Medhurst'a back and
killed him instantly. His ribs were crushed
in and it is believed the bones pressed
upon his heart. \u25a0
Martin was thrown from the wagon and
struck on his head. A heavy piece of
furniture fell upon him, breaking several
ribs and injuring him internally. Dr.
Champion, whd is attending him. says
there is but little chance of his living.
Charles Medhurst came to Colton from
Iowa thirteen years ago. He was 63 years
of age. To-morrow would have been his
birthday, and his aged wife had prepared
a surprise for him in the nature of a
family reunion and a birthday dinner.
"SOONEHS" WILL HAVE
CHANCE AT THE LAND
Old System Likely to Be Adopted at
Opening of Two Indian
Reservations.
"WASHINGTON, Oct. 1.â The Interior
Department is rapidly completing plans
for the opening of the Fort Hall (Idaho)
â¢Indian reservation. The date 'for the
opening has not yet been fixed, as the
preliminary work has not been completed,
but it is expected that the reservation,
which contains 400.000 acres, will be
thrown open to settlement within a few
weeks.
The Quinault reservation in "Washing
ton, comprising 200,000 acres, probably will
be thrown open to settlement next spring.
The contract for surveying the reserva
tion is about to be awarded. Commis
sioner Herman of the General Land Of
fice said to-day that it was probable that
the old "sooner" system would be adopted
at the opening of both these reservations.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN DIEGO, Oct. 1.â There were devel
opments of an interesting nature in. the
Chinese smuggling cases to-day, and after
telegraphic communication between the
Collector's office in this city and the Dis
trict Attorney's office in Los Angeles the
order came to place Tom "Wing Chew,
alias Lee Cheong, under arrest. The Mon
golian has been indicted already for aid
ing and abetting Chinese in their efforts
to avoid the exclusion laws and was out
under $l00o bail, with a Chinese as surety.
The discoveries of this morning- make it
evident that he was concerned in the
bringing into this country of three Chi
nese v/no were arrested on Saturday, two
of whom were ordered deported on Mon
day. Collector Bowers and Chinese In
spector You went to the jail and searched
tne effects of the Chinese and found full
directions from Lee Cheong as to how
they should act on arriving in Los An
geles, to which city they were purchas
ing tickets when arrested. The letters
were to Chinese in Los Angeles who were
to meet the three when they reached the
Angel City, and they are all signed by Lee
Cheong.
In the letters it was said that the bear
ers were strangers in the* country and
should be taken care of until they could
be sent forward to San Francisco. There
was also a copy of a telegram which the
Los Angeles Chinese was to send tt> Lee
Cheong' s brother in San Francisco as
soon as the three were satisfactorily
started for the metropolis.
On the request of District Attorney Val
entine at Los Angeles, Lee Cheong's bail
was raised in the. former case from $1000
to $25,000 by the Federal court and Depu
ty United States Marshal Place was di
rected to take him into custody. He was
found in Chinatown and made an ineffect
uul effort to escape. A new complaint
will be sworn to in the morning by Col
lector Bowers, charging Cheong with aid
ing and abetting the advewx or the three
Mongolians and, if held, there will be
another case for the Federal Grand Jury.
The three Chinese are supplied with
fraudulent certificates of residence in this
country, all of which purport to have
been made out and signed in 1894, when
all Chinese were compelled to register.
These certificates were supposed to give
some of the characteristic marks of the
Chinese registered and to carry the photo
graphs of the men. The seal of the In
ternal Revenue Collector was. so placed
that it stamped the photograph as well
as the certificate, and it was evidently
thought that this wo.uld prevent the
changing of the photographs. Chinese die
as well as other people, and it is claimed
by the customs officials that the certifi
cates of the dead as well as of those who
have returned to China to remain are kept
for use by such other Chinese as may de
sire to come over. Old photographs are
soaked off and new ones substituted.
In order to get around the matter of the
seal, a bogus seal has been made. It is
a facsimile Of "O. M. "Welburn, Internal
Revenue Collector," who was in charge of
the revenue district of California when
the certificates were first issued.

Special Plates
The principal subject discussed was
that of a cable to Hawaii. Guam and the
Philippines. Propositions have been made
to lay a. commercial cable from San Fran
cisco to connect these islands, and tho
question under discussion was whether
under our peace treaty with 'Spain the
United States could authorize or In any
way encourage the layfng of such a cable
by private parties.
Under a franchise obtained from Spain
some time before the late war the ex
clusive right to cable connections with
the Philippines was secured by a foreign
corporation. Under the treaty of Paris
the United States obligated itself to pro
tect all property rights in the archipel
ago, and the question now at Issue Is
whether permission to land the proposed
cable at Manila or some other Philippine
port would be a violation of the terms of
the Paris treaty.
The Attorney General will prepare a
statement for the President covering all
the questions involved.

CAB LE PROJECT
State Chairman "William* Gurley was
made permanent chairman without oppo
sition. . Resolutions were confined to State
issues. The following nominations for
Governor were made: Ex-Congressman
Thomas M. Ferrell of Glosboro, Mayor
James M. Seymour of Newark, ex-Judge
Howard Carrow of Camden, - Senator
Christian Braun of Passaic and James E.
Martins of Union.
The anti-Seymour people made an effort
to concentrate their strength on Ferrell
and succeeded to the extent of securing
480 votes for him on the first ballot, which
was only eight less than the number
given to Seymour. On the second ballot
the scattering vote went largely to Sey
mour and accomplished his nomination.
The first ballot stood: Seymour, 488; Fer
rell, 480; Braun, 51; Carrow. 68; Martln3,
22; necessary to choice, 574. On the second
ballot Seymour gained fifty-six- votes,
most of which came through his getting
the vote of Morris and Warren counties.
Ferrell lost only eleven votes, but Sey
mour was within thirty votes of nomina
tion, and then began a general stampede.
When the vote was finally announced it
stood 779 for Seymour, with the remaining
votes for Ferrel!.
TRENTON, N. J. f Oct. 1.â Mayor Sey
mour of Newark was nominated on the
second ballot to-day aa the Democratic
candidate for Governor in a convention
that at times was turbulent in the ex
treme. State Chairman William Gurley
was permanent chairman. The first voting
Bhowed that the Seymour and anti-Sey
mour forces were almost evenly divided,
and had tho anti-Seymour leaders been
able to concentrate their vote on a single
candidate they might .have won. How
ever, there was a strong undercurrent
among the delegates for Seymour.
The nomination of "Mayor Seymour was
a defeat for former United States Senator
Smith and his forces and was. a victory
for E. F. C. Young, Robert Davis of Hud
son County and Colonel E. I* Price of
Essex County. \u25a0 4 -
Ex-Judge Robert 'Woodruff of Mercer,
temporary chairman of the convention,
made feeling reference to the late Presi
dent McKinley, and when he finished
speaking the band played "Nearer, My
God, to Thee." The vast assemblage rose
and sang the words of the hymn.
The first tumult of the convention broke
out when both of tho chairmen of con
testing delegations from Essex County
tried to name the county's representatives
on the several committees. After the con
vention/ had been in an uproar for some
time and the lle^had been freely passed
Chairman Woodruff permitted E. W.
tjrane, the Democratic candidate of three
years ago for Governor and an anti-Sey
mourites, to name the Essex representa
tives on the committee on credentials and
order of business.

CAPTAIN SIGSBEE EXPLAINS.
Officer Who Commanded the Maine
Takes the Witness Stand.
Captain Charles G. Slgabee. who commanded
the scout ship St. Paul during the Spanish
war, was next called. He said that in obedi
ence to orders from Captain Wise, wlio was
his commanding officer, he had proceeded to
the vicinity of Santiago, arriving there on
the 21st. He told of taking aboard the Cuban
pilot, Nunez, and said that he did not have
great confidence in that individual. His In
structions were to report to Commodore Schley
that the Spanish squadron probably was In
Santiago harbor. He fell in with the flying
squadron on the evening of May 26, the squad
ron then being twenty or twenty-five miles
south of Santiago. He had reported to Com
modore Schley that he "knew nothing posi
tively" about the Spanish fleet. He was then
asked if he had expressed his belief to Com
modore Schley that Cervera's fleet wan not in
the harbor as reported later by Commodore

sibility of the question and the court took

All jockeys and trainers will be , re
quired to make application for license
from the New California Jockey Club.
This- condition is enforced by all other
turf bodies in America and other coun
tries. Jockeys and trainers will not be
permitted to pursue their respective vo
cations on 'the California tracks until
such license Is granted.
It was determined to insert the claim
ing clause in half the selling purses and
to eliminate it from the others, thus giv
ing horsemen an opportunity to exercise
their choice in such races.
The directors of the New California
Jockey Club met yesterday and decided
to open the forthcoming season at Oak
land on November 2. It was the original
Intention to I commence proceedings at
Tanforan. The change Is made to ac
commodate .the large number of horses
now quartered here and en route from
the East, as the track across the bay has
greater facilities in the matter of stab-
Iing and other details than has Tanforan.
Ihe racing will continue about thirtv
days, when the scene of operations wiil
be shifted to Tanforan. >
The open season for ducks and email
was ushered in auspiciously yesterday
by a great throng of sportsmen although
the results were not up to expectations.
Throughout the length of the marsh line
from above Suisun on the north to Al
viso on the south the glistening barrels
of shotguns reflected the too aruent rays
of the sun.
The sport was fairly good on the Al
viso marsh, every one getting some birds.
Few secured the limit bag of fifty birds,
although George Pierce v/as among the
successful ones. Fred King brought home
forty-six. The bags were maue up of
teal, curlew, rail, snipe, sprig and spoon
bills. A number | of sportsmen returned
with well-lilled game bags last night and
reported that many more- remained over
%o try the sport this morning.
â¢ Up at Suisun marshes the season was
opened early in the morning with rather
poor sport. Few good bags were made
although plenty of ammunition was used.
The weather was sultry, with no wind
j blowing, so the few ducks Hying went
too high for good shooting. The best
score of the . aay was made by Walter
Cooper, a sixteen-year-old lad, on Stew
art ponds. He bagged forty-nine ducks.
The next largest scores were H. Perkins
forty. A. Anderson thirty-seven and J.
C. Murphy twenty-five, all of the Suisun
Gun Club. The members of the club
killed a total of 200 ducks. Judge W R
Wells of Contra Cotta County, a guest'
bagged fourteen. : '
Frank Maskey, E. F. Preston, L. D
Owens, H. Rose and W. Hubert were '
not up early enough and missed the
cream of the shooting. The combined
bag was thirty-eight birds. They have
the shooting privilege of the famous
Prlngle ponds, which affords the best
canvasback shooting .to be had any
where.
Herman Oelrichs returned last evening
with few birds. J
Colonel H. I. Kowalsky and Samuel
Hughes shot over ponds owned by J. N
Kowalsky, east, of Suisun. They bagged
fourteen ducks and eighteen snipe.
H. Herzer and C. A. Morrlll of this city
bagged forty-five ducks on the Morrill
preserve. - - \u25a0 .

sun spent three days on Grizzly Island
and returned with only twenty-two birds.

lumbia following immediately. Once again
the American boat showed her superior
smartness In stays, coming" about much
quicker than her rival and gathering way
as soon as her head sheets were trimmed
down. At 11:30 both yachts were pretty
nearly becalmed, the Columbia having a
lead of about a quarter of a mile. At
11:30 the Shamrock went on the starboard
tack, the Columbia following, and then a
lucky streak filled the Shamrock's sall3
and gave her an appreciable lift. At noon
both yachts were on the port tack, almost
becalmed. The club topsails and jib top
sails were the only sails that puffed out
responsive to the stray streaks of air.
The big mainsails napped as the yachts
rose and fell in the long easterly roll and
the luff of the headaails Quivered. .The
result was that- the yachts barely had
steerage way.
The Unexpected Happens.
Then the unexpected happened. Wafted
along at a fine clip by a stray puff, the
Shamrock overhauled the Columbia and
at 1:15 she was so close that Captain Barr
bore down on her in order to kill her
wind, as he had so often killed the wind
of the Constitution. The Shamrock went
on the port tack, immediately followed by
the Columbia, and in another minute the
The wind now became more south
erly. Both yachts set reaching
jib 'topsails and eased off sheets,
steering a direct . course for the
mark. Then the Shamrock forged ahead
and got into the doldrums, while the Co
lumbia got a slant of wind and at 1:85
passed the Shamrock. Then It was the
Shamrock's turn to get the benefit of a
breeze and she came along and planted
herself on the Columbia's weather quar
ter, crawling up on her until at 2:08 they
were neck and neck, with the British boat
to the windward, blanketing her op
ponent. To escape from this blanket Cap
tain Barr sent the Columbia on the port
tack at 2:32, but luck was against her and
she lost every breath of the fickle wind,
as well as steerage way, and it was near
ly two minutes before she got away and
filled away on the starboard tack again
and by that time the Shamrock was too
far ahead to be overtaken. The first
mark was then close at hand and the
yachts tacked around it as follows:
Shamrock .'... 2:41:36
Columbia 2:44:44

three minutes and twenty seconds In the
first ten miles, and try as the Columbia
would, she was unable to lessen the wide
g-ap that \u25a0 seoarated her from her an
tagonist She held her luff, hunting for
wind, but, falling to find It. dropped slow

MAY RACE CONSECUTIVE DAYS.
Meeting Iakely to Be Held to Con
sider This Subject.
NEW YORK, Oct. 1.â E. D. Morgan,
who. with a party of friends, remained
for the night aboard the Park City inside
Sandy Hook,' said to-night:
"To-day's race cannot be discussed from
the point of a critic. It was merely a
succession of flukes and never resembled
a real race. The challenger merely was
more fortunate than the Columbia In
catching the fitful puffs of wind. I regret
extremely the outcome because we were
nil anxious to see what the boats would
do on the triangular course. But as it Is
we are now no wiser than before. So far
as I am able to say there will be no
change in the defender's sails. The pres
ent suit 13 quite satisfactory."
Captain Barr was extremely reticent.
Absolutely no expression could be ob
tained from him upon any point further
than regret over the miserable racing
weather.
While there was no meeting of the New-
York Yacht Club to-night of either the
challenge or the regatta : committee, it
was Intimated that the cup committee of
the club and the representatives of the
Royal Ulster Club might have a meeting
in the near future with regard to having
the yachts raced on consecutive days.
This was done two years ago, after a
number of postponements because of the
lack of wind, similar to the conditions
which prevailed to-day. It requires an
agreement on' the part of both sides to
have the races sailed on consecutive
days, and it is likely that another post
ponement may bring this about. It Is
possible also that some sort of meeting
may be held to-morrow. The next race,
however, will not be- sailed until Thurs
day, although there may be a race Fri
day. . â¢ â¢ \u25a0

NEW YORK, Oct. 1.â To-day's at
tempt to sail the second of the
present series Of international
yacht races for the blue ribbon
of the sea proved a dismal fail
ure. Between 25,000 and 30,000 people who
crowded on the pleasure fleet off Sandy
Hook lightship in the hope of seeing a
repetition of : the thrilling sport of last
Saturday witnessed instead more of a
drifting match than a race. The wind
was exceedingly light and variable, at
times falling so low that the gossamer
wind pennants, which are as light as
thistledown, hung limp against the masts
of the big single-stickers. The wind, with
crews lined up on the lee rails, was not
sufficient at any time to make the racing
machines heel to their lines.
At the end of four and a half hours the
two yachts covered less than one-half of
the prescribed course' of thirty miles, and
as there was no possibility of their fin
ishing within the time limit the regatta
committee declared the race off. When
the gun was fired announcing this deci
sion the challenger was about a half-mile
ahead of the defender, and to that extent
to-day's trial was a victory for the Sham
rock.
Dangerous in Light Airs.
But the fluke to-day demonstrated little
as to the question of .supremacy between
the two boats except, perhaps, that Sir
Thomas' new champion Is more danger-,
ous in light airs thanwas supposed after
her two former meetings with the white
liyer, to which the patriots are pinning
their faith. In tluky winds of variable
strength, shifting as they did to-day
through six points of the compass, luck
cuts almost as much figure as model and
seamanship, ani to-day fortune was un
mistakably with the foreigner. '
The course was a equilateral triangle,
ten miles to the leg. the first leg being a
beat due east into the wind. The Yankee
skipper at the start outgeneraled his ad
versary in a splendid piece of Jockeying,
crossing the line in the windward bertn
twelve seconds ahead of â¢ the Britisher.
This advantage tne Columbia held for
two hours, while both yachts steered far
off the course to the southward, looking
for a streak of wind that would pront
them.
Finally Barr declined longer to continue
the vainless quest. He put his helm down
and headed inshore on the starboard tack.
The Shamrock at this time, after both
had been sailing in the same airs, was a
beaten boat, and her skipper could af
ford to take a gambler's chance with for
tune. He held doggedly on and fortune
smiled on him. Within five minutes he
got a breeze out of the south which ruf
fied the crestless seas and wafted him like
a ghost through the Columbia's weather.
But this was not his best piece of good
fortune. After this reverse the Columbia,
slipping through the seas at an astonish
ing pace, had worked out ahead of the
Shamrock, but to the leeward. When 'the
two vachts were In this position, about
two miles from the first turn, a cant of
the wind threatened to blanket the Colum
bia and to avfcid such a possibility Barr
went under the Shamrock's stern. Just
as he did so the wind hauled around
more to the south, knocking the Colum
bia's head off, until to the astonished
spectators, she seemed headed almost
back to the lightship at the- starting line.
That golden boat, favored by the same
breeze, was headed in exactly the oppo
site direction. Then the wind shifted
back again and when Barr got the Colum
bia straightened out he was a third of a
mile astern. In this position the two
boats rounded the first mark, the Sham
rock three minutes and eight seconds be
fore the Columbia, or a gain for the
Shamrock in the beat to windward, adding
the twelve seconds which the Columbia
beat her over the line, of three minutes
and twenty seconds.
Race Is Declared Off.
During the next hour. In a close reach
for the second mark, the yachts were able
to cover about four or four and a half
miles of the remaining twenty, and as.
only fifty minutes then remained before
the expiration of the time limit, the race
was declared off.
After turning the mark the experts
thought the Shamrock increased her lead
slightly. This gain may, however, be ac
counted for by the fact that she carried a
large reaching sail, while the Columbia
was working close to the wind with a
small baby jib topsail, a mere handker
chief compared to the Shamrock's larger
sail.
When the excursion fleet . headed back
for home the only real race of the day oc
curred. It was the race for New York,
i To-night both the Yankees and the for-
I eigners are praying for a good, stiff
I breeze on Thursday, when the next race
will be sailed. . .
When the string of signals was hoisted
on the committee boat indicating the
course the rival racers were circling
about the" lightship, â¢ performing 'nearly
every known variety of marine maneuv
ers. The Shamrock was clothed in a
brand new suit of light weather sails, ad
mirably cut and of marvelous fit. . The
Columbia, however, was obliged to con
tent herself with the same old suit that
carried her to victory on Saturday. ' Be
fore the start the Shamrock hoisted a
tiny jib topsail, perhaps the smallest ever
; carried by a cup yacht. It answered Its
| purpose capitally as a steering sail ami
i proved of great advantage in a day so
j prolific of flukes and doldrums.- 'j

j'At that time both yachts were on the
port tack, standing to the southward,
I with the Shamrock in the lead and the
I Columbia following in her wake. Ten
minutes later the bang of the warning
gun was heard and' the' Columbia, wear
ing sharply round, 1 trimmed her sails- on
the other tack, the Shamrock crossing her
bows.'- The Columbia took good care, to
secure the weather .berth, and ten seconds
after the starting gun, which was fired at
the dot of 11, she crossed the 'line- on the

ward. The Shamrock luffed over the line

A JEW YORK, Oct. I. Always optimistic, Sir Thomas Upton to-day, after the race, was in
1 V the best of spirits. He said:
?/ really don't see anything yet on which to base a definite opinion regarding the two
boats. While the Shamrock was leading when the race was called off, there's nothing to brag
about To-day while the winds were very light, there was little Unking, therein differing from last
Thursday's breezes, which, in spots, failed utterly, each boat in turn striking dead calms. After the
shozvino made thus far by both boats, I should not hesitate if I were a betting man to lay even money
on the Shamrock. Both "boats were handled to-day to the best ability by the best men each coun
try could produce. I don't think there's a better skipper in America than Captain Barr of the Co
lumbia."
Sir Thomas expressed himself as personally in favor of racing every day, provided any sug
gestion looking to that end should be made.
Captain Bob Wringe/who stands by Sir Thomas on the bridge, when asked if he considered
Shamrock's chances better, said:
"I can't say that her chances are better, for they were never bad."

The trial of Patrick Clancy, special
officer, on the charge of murder for shoot
ing and killing Attorney J. N. E. Wilson
on May 27 last, was continued before a
jury In Judge Lawlor's court yesterday.
From the questions put to the witnesses
on cross examination it is apparent that
a claim of self defense will be made and
probably temporary Insanity. , -
The witnesses examined were Dr. Lcuis
D. Bacigalupi, who made the autopsy on
Wilson's body; Samuel H. Bookheim, who
heard the fatal shots; Dr. Cecil M. Armi
stead, who attended Wilson at i tha Re
ceiving Hospital; T. I. Fitzpatrick, assist
ant warrant clerk, who took Wilson's
dying statement; Henry F. Young, who
found the envelope on which Wilson had
written that Clancy had shot him; Police
man Nelsen, who arrested Clancy and
huard his admission that he had killed
Wilson, and Detective T. L. Ryan and
Captain Seymour.

A High Church Champion.
Among the prominent speakers who will
take part in the convention is Right Rev.
Isaac I>>a Nicholson, Bishop of Milwau
kee, who is another of those prelates of
his church who sympathize with the rit
ualistic movement and was one of the
s< ven who participated in the noted Fond
du Lac consecration. He is a native of
Baltimore and began life as a banker.
Entering Dartmouth, he became the cen
ter of un association of students, among
whom were Bishops Leonard and Talbot,
and by whom he was influenced to take
Episcopal orders. He studied at the Vir
ginia Theological Seminary and was
made priest in 1S72. Subsequently called
to the rectorship of old St. Mark's. Phila
delphia, he developed into a priest of the
highest ability. Chosen to the diocese of
Wisconsin, he has extended the church
and its institutions to a wide extent.
Thrice divided, at another convention an
additional diocese will be created.
Bishop Nicholson is a firm advocate of
the church as a vita!, living reality. He
will follow in the footsteps of Bishops
Grsfton and Seymour, the high church
etiamjjions.
Is an Advanced Churchman.
The great question of ritualism in the
church and the stand which may be taken
on the subject by the convention Is ab
sorbing the attention of the laity and
clergy throughout the country. One of
the most famous advocates of advanced
thurohmanshij) is the Right Rev. George
Franklin Seymour, Bishop of Springfield,
in the province of Illinois.
Bishop Seymour by birth Is a New
Yorker, and a noted graduate of Colum
bia. He studied for the ministry* at the
General Theological Seminary and was
ordained priest in 1S53. A scholar by at
tainment, he is a natural educator. He
v.as the flr^t warden of St. Stephen's Col
lege; a training school for candidates for
the ministry. At one time he. was rector
of St. John's Cnurch. Brooklyn, but re
slgnt-d to become professor of ecclesiasti
cal history in the General Theological
Srminary. He was once called to Grace
Church in th!s city, but declined. H's
high church views cost him the election
to the episcopate of Missouri by a vote of
one.
He- was elected Bishop of Illinois, but
the house of deputies refused to confirm
In 1S77 he was unanimously elected Bish
op of Springfield after a famous contro
versy, but declined. Again he was chosen
and accepted. He was consecrated in
Trinity Church, N>w York, to the sorrow
of the low church element, who were
pure that the church would become
Roman right away. In this they were
disappointed. Dr. Seymour is a great
preacher and an aggressive controversial
ist. He is sure to be a most interesting
"sure in the convention. As a. diocesan
bis administration has been wise and suc
ctssful.

In 1S95 the firm of Drexel, Morgan & Co.
became J. P. Morgan & Co., which is J.
Pierpont Morgan and eleven others who*.,
he has associated with him, not for their
financial strength, but for their assistance
as lieutenants in handling the vast
amount of business that has been drawn
to the house, which now, though a private
concern. Is one of the most powerful
financial factors in the business of the
world.
Mr. Morgan's father died in 1S90, leav
ing him a fortune of some $10,000,000 and
the sole control of the London house of
J. S. Morgan & Co. and the Paris house
of Morgan, Harjes & Co., with all their
worldwide connections.
From the outset of his career Mr. Mor
gan paid especial attention to the "foreign
exchange" department of the banking
business, and having in time thoroughly
mastered it was in a position of advant
age in securing a large share of the
enormous business in that line necessi
tated by our large and ever growing im
port and export trade.
In 1S60 John Pierpont Morgan became
the American agent for George Peabody
& Co., London; four years later he organ
ized the firm of Dabney, Morgan & Co.,
and in 1871 became connected with the
wealthy Drexels of Philadelphia In the
firm of Drexel, Morgan & Co.
Joseph Morgan, Mr. Morgan's grand
father, took part in the Revolution. As a
farmer and tavern-keeper at Hartford he
amassed what was then counted a good
fortune. His lands occupied a large part
of Asylum Hill and In time became quite
valuable. This property was left to his
son Junius, John Pierpont Morgan's
father, who by energy and natural apti
tude for business, first as a dry goods
merchant and then as the associate of
George Peabody, the great banker, mul
tiplied it into millions and laid part of the
foundation for the vast fortune that has
been gathered by his son.
John Pierpont Morgan was born in
Hartford, Conn., where he attended school
until he was 14, when his father moved
to Boston. There he became a student at
the celebrated Knglish High School and
on his graduation therefrom at the age
of 18 spent two years at the University
of Gottingen, Germany. ...
At the age of 21 he entered the banking
house of Duncan, Sherman & Co., New
York.
On his father's side his family dates
back to 1636, when Miles Morgan, the
first of the line on this side of the water,
landed on New England soil and with a
few others founded the town of Spring
field, Mass.
Mr. Morgan isaNewEnglander by birth
and ancestry. His mother, from whom he
gets his middle name, was Miss Juliet,
daughter of .the Rev. John Pierpont, the
author of the stirring little poem on the
appeal supposed to have been made by
Avarren to nismenatthe battle of Bunker
Hill, which begins "Stand! The ground's
your own, my braves.' Mr. Morgan inher
ited something of his grandfather's poet
ical nature, and in his youth did several
things in verse, to which, however, he
does not claim- to owe much of his pres
ent fame.
It Is the same In the vestry meetings at
St. George's, where Mr. Morgan is senior
warden of the church, and where as such
he can be seen at the Sunday service
passing' the plate for offerings of the con
gregation, always adding a comparatively
liberal contribution, if not the scriptur
ally enjoined tithe.
With his pastor, Rev. W. S. Rainsford,
who came to St. George's in 1881, when
quite a young man, Mr. Morgan has al
ways been on the most intimate terms
and given him the most hearty support
and co-operation in his work for the tem
poral and spiritual betterment of. the peo
ple within reach.
Besides a, new parish house for St.
George's, which Mr. Morgan erected at
a cost of Â¥300,000, the New York Trade
School, which he established at a cost of
$500,000; the new Lying-in Hospital, to
which he gave $1,350,000; the Loomis Con
sumption Hospital, rome $500,000, aÂ«d
$500,000 to the Young Men's Christian As
sociation, $1,000,000 to Harvard University,
$125,000 for the preservation of the Pali
sades of the Hudson, $100,000 to a library
for Holyoke, Mass., where his father was
born, and other gifts too numerous for
special mention, Mr. Morgan gives each
year considerably over $100,000 to the reg
ular church work and individual charities
of New York and the vicinity.
Inherits a Practical Nature.
period of twenty-one years, he has been in
his place as delegate and performed most
valuable service on the committees to
which he has been appointed.
Always a regular attendant at the ses
sions of the convention, an attentive lis
tener and taking a keen interest in the
proceedings, yet his voice is seldom heard
on the floor. When it is, whether speak
ing to the question or making a commit
tee report, there is never any doubt as to
his meaning.
Never taning upon himself the proper
duty. of the others, his purse ever opens
to supplement their efforts, or to bridge
over un emergency when the need comes.
Yet, with it all, in this one field of ac
tion Mr. Morgan performs his duty and
pleasure (for such duty is to him) without
seeking to dominate, as perforce of cir
cumstnnce and surroundings he does in
the business world.

Before Congressman Kahn went from
this city to the islands he conferred with
merchants who wished to get special in
formation on certain points. â¢ He accord
ingly made inquiries for their benefit. The
new revenue law for the Philippines pro
mulgated by the War Department has
been put into shape, and opinions of the
merchants of Luzon were sought as to
the effect Its operation will have upon
trade with the United States.
Congressman Shafroth, who has been
studying the business possibilities of the
Philippines, also returned on the Thomas.
On the same vessel were Surgeon Gen
eral Stemberg and Commissary General
Weston, who went to the islands upon an
official tour of Inspection, also General
Kobbe and Postmaster Stocking of Ma
nila.
Congressman Julius Kahn arrived yes
terday from the Philippines on the trans
port Thomas. He was accompanied by
his wife. Congressman Kahn went to the
Philippines several months ago to Inves
tigate for himself, as a lawmaker, the
conditions existing in the American pos
sessions. While he was in the south seas
he visited all the principal islands of the
Philippine group and studied the products
of the land and also considered the needs
of the natives, which may call for sup
plies from, the United States, and particu
larly from San Francisco. In Manila he
was banqueted by the Chamber of Com
merce, and many military and civic of
ficers were present at the banquet. Jus
tice James F. Smith of the Philippine Su
preme Court presided and A. W. Fergu
son acted as toastmasttr.

The chairman of the missionary exhibit
is Mrs. Sydney M. Van Wyck. The chair
man of the heads of courts is Mrs. L. F.
Comstock. the secretary is Mrs. N. Li.
Nokes and the treasurer is Mrs. W. G.
Morrow. \u25a0 . \u25a0
The exhibits will be classified under
distinct heads. That of China will be in
charge of Mrs. John F. Swift. Mexico
will be presided over by Miss Gwin. Afri
ca by Miss Alice Brown. North American
Indians by Mrs. Thurlow McMullin. Phil
ippines and West Indies by Mrs. Frances
B. Edperton. Brazil by Mrs. Thomas L.
Thompson. Japan by Miss Philip Caduc,
Alaska by Miss Sally Maynard and col
ored from the South by Mrs. J. Goddard
Clark.
The missionary loan committee consists
of Mrs. Daniel MeLeod. Mrs. George E..
Wellington. Mrs. Robert Waller and Mrs.
F. G. Sanborn. The press agents are
Mrs. W.i C Morrow and Miss Bertha
Monroe Rickoff.
The work of preparing th? booths is a
laborious one, but it is making rapid pro
gress. The exhibit will open Monday next
and continue during the convention.
Creation of New Diocese.
The utmost interest in the proceedings
of the general convention centers in the
subject of the creation of new dioceses
and divisions of the old. The -church is
growing by leaps and bounds, and more
and more the ilmlt of endurance of the
Bishops is reached. The only relief that
is regarded as practicable is by the mul
tiplication of O.ioceses. Coadjutor Bish
ops, except in cases where the Bishop is
disabled on account of age or physical
infirmity, are not regarded as a desirable
alternative. The danger of a conflict of
authority is ever present. The remedy
seems to be in the creation of new dio
ceses. Massachusetts will be one of the
dioceses that will he cut in half, and the
new diocese of Western Massachusetts
will come into being well equipped in
every respect. The churchmen of Boston
have raised $l:X),000 as an endowment of
the new diocese, which will embrace the
larger portions of the territory of the old
Bay State, but not numerically In the
number of communicants or clergy. West
ern Massachusetts being an independent
jurisdiction will elect its own Bishop as
soon as the consent of the church to the
measure is gained.
The diocese of Olympia, embracing the
cities 6f Seattle and Tacoma, including
the prosperous region of Western Wash
ington and Puget Sound, has sent a strong
delegation to secure an independent or
ganization. It is promised to raise $30,000
in six years as an endowment. The Bish
ops are loth to accept promises to pay.
If Olympia churchmen had raised $20,000
in cash the consent of the church to its
independent organization would have been
assured. Olympia must consent to remain
a missionary jurisdiction for the present.
The utmost consideration will be given
to the subject of provisional Bishops for
the Philippines. Porto Rico and Cuba. If
the church is to find a place for itself in
the new possessions a Bishop for each is
an absolute necessity. The question will
be quickly decided in the affirmative if
the means of support can bo assured.
The care of the church in Hawaii can
be settled in but one way â by the resig
nation of Bishop Willis. He cannot be
superseded. If the" present Bishop will
step down and out the American church
will soon settle the auestion.
The convention therefore lias the ap
pointment oj! several new Bishops on ita
hands: North Dakota, to succeed Bishop
Edsall. transferred to Minnesota; Olym
\u25a0pia, as successor to Bishop Parker; Porto
Rico. Cuba and the Philippines, besides
Hawaii.
Whether or not Bishop Rowe will con
tinue in Alaska depends .upon his support.
J. Pierpont Morgan guaranteed the Bish
op's salary for the first three years. If
he continues this benefaction the church
in Alaska will retain its present hard
worked and devoted head. Upon whom
the choice for Bishops will be placed agi
tates many of the younger element of the
clergy.
Programme for To-Day.
The opening service of the General Con
vention will take place this morning at 11
at Trinity Church. Bush and Gough
streets. The service will be participated
In by all the Bishops and the clerical arid
lay delegates to the convention. It will
consist of the holy communion office, and
the sermon will be preached by the Right
Rev. the Bishop of Oregon, B. Wistar
Morris.
At 3 this afternoon the House of Bish
ops and the House of Delegates will con
vene and organize for business. The
Bishops will meet in executive session,
while the House of Deputies will be called
to order by the Rev. Charles Hutchings,
secretary of the house. After the roll has
been called and the credentials of the
delegates have been presented the house
will proceed to elect a chairman. In the
absence of the Rev. Morgan Dlx of New
York City, who is unable to be present,
there will be many candidates for the
honor of presiding over the deliberations
of the house.

progress that has been made among these
aborigines is one of the boasts of the
church.

t Legal Holidays boats and trains will run on i
Sunday time. _ "'."Â«-- rtIn making this salute you must bear in
mind that you are to salute with the
hand that is opposite to the person you
wish to greet. Thus, if he is to pass you
on your , left, you use the right hand,
and if he .passes you on your right you
must employ the left hand.
It consists ofthese three simple move
ments: .First, when the person is about;
seven paces away: you throw either the
right or the left arm. as the case may. be,
easily and gracefully up in front of you.;
so the upper arm is perpendicular to the
body and the forearm is obliquely across
the body, so that the. fingers touch the
rim of your hat near the front and center
of the face;, the hand is open, the â¢palm
being nearest the face, and the. J fingers
are close together and slightly bent., with
the thumb hidden by being in front of
the .first finger and nearest, the . face.
Second, you incline s.lightly and remove
your hand about four Inches in front of
your face. Third,, when the person, has
passed you, you straighten up and thro-*
your arm smartly at the side. You may
then continue your walk, possessed of
the gratified feeling" , of \u25a0 having been
strictly "in the mode," and of having
greeted your friend with : a < natural,
graceful, salute. . ,
Latest in Salutes.
RI6HT TO BEAR ARMSâ CUizen, City.
The right to bear arms is guaranteed by
the constitution of the United States. That
has been construed to bear arms openly.
The laws or ordinances that prohibit the
carrying of weapons concealed has been
held not to be in conflict with that pro
vision of . tha constitution. The law
against carrying weapons concealed Is
based on the theory that a man who car
ries weapons concealed desires to take un-'
fair advantage of an antagonist or to
commit a cowardly assault. The law
granting permits to carry weapons con
cealed is based on the theory that those
to whom such permits are issued are
law-abiding citizens, who carry arms for
self-protection only.

GUN AND TRAINâ E. W. B., Milpitas,
Cal. "If a man standing on the rear end cf
a train moving at the rate of a mile a
minute discharged a gun. the bullet from
which moved at the rate of a mile a min
ute in a direction opposite from the train,
would the bullet move from the gun or
shoot any distance?'* is the question
asked by this correspondent. If the gun
wes in proper condition, the powder fault
less and the shot fired, the bullet would
go to the rear as far as the elevation of
the gun and the force of the powder
would send it.

ANSWERS TO QUERIES.
A CHANCE TO SMILE.
YESTERDAY The Call had again to record an
affray caused by an assault upon a workingman
in the peaceable pursuit of his work. This time
*he assaulted man had not only a weapon, but the
courage to use it, and it was not he who suffered
most from the affray. The affair, however, is deplor
able. Here are two mere men seriously wounded as a
result of the attacks and assaults which are now becom
ing so frequent in the city. The controversy is clearly
approaching the inevitable climax, and if there be not
a vigorous enforcement of the law and an arrest of the
lawless we shall very soon have serious rioting, in
which many lives will be lost.
The men who are now seeking to terrorize the peace
able workingmen of the city are growing bolder and
are relying more and more constantly upon the sup
port given them by the Examiner and its allies. The
incitements to riot which that paper has been publish
ing increase in fierceness and in falseness. Over and
over again these mischief-making inciters to strife call
upon workingmen to resist the police and the law.
The effect of their appeals to the violent and the
lawless is only too apparent. We arc passing from
the period of assaults committed by scattered gangs
to dangerous affrays in which large numbers take
part. Lawlessness is clearly upon the verge of an
open outburst, and yet the yellow journal and its
special commissioners continue to vilify the police,
the city government, the merchants, and, in fact,
every law-abiding element of the community.
In one of the inflammatory articles published in the
Examiner of yesterday and written by one of its
special commissioners it was said: "There is a secret
body of wealthy men unknown to the constitution
and above the law who have full power over an armed
force of about 1289 men â a fair-sized army. * * *
This army holds the city. No man is safe., from it.
JBy night and day attacks are made upon peaceable
citizens; unoffending wayfarers are shot down in
the public streets. * * \u2666 Never did the tyrants
of Greece, never did the despots of the medieval
cities, never did the Czar of Russia dare to deal with
their subjects so."
Statements of that kind, wild and silly as they are,
have yet a potent influence when addressed to men
already inflamed against the law and ready to strike
at any point where they believe there is a chance to
inflict an injury without getting hurt themselves. The
appeals may not be deliberately designed as incite
ments to riot, but they unquestionably have- that ten
dency. They are poured out day after day as incen
tives to action on the part of all who are opposed
to the law, who are antagonistic to the police and
who would turn the city over to riot and pillage if
they dared to do it.
In the face of the repeated assaults and affrays the
supporters of the lawless elements have the effrontery
to assert that the city would be peaceable were it not
. for the police. . They declare that the city government
and the business men are responsible for all the blood
that has been shed in the numerous attacks that have
been made since the disturbances began. The object
of all these falsehoods and slanders is to hold the law
ful authorities of the municipality up to the scorn of
the violent and the vicious.
It was just such falsehoods directed by the Exam
iner day after day against McKinley that led to his
assassination. From equal causes there will follow
equal effects. We have had assaults and affrays. Are
we now to have assassinations and riots as a result of
the teaching of the Examiner and its allies?
To the Mayor as the chief magistrate of the city
the people look in this emergency. It devolves upon
him to protect the workingmfcn of the city from the
strikers who assail them, and to so strengthen the
police that the lawless cannot assail it .with impunity.
HASTENING TO THE END.
THE automobile writes its autograph on the faces of Its
devotees just as legibly as football, six-day cycle
contests, golf, a lunatic asylum or the Eastern peni
tentiary, says the North American. Football, it Is well
known, brings about a facial resemblance to a bat-eared bull
dog; cycle contests mark the face with deep lines and give the
eyes a tendency to pop out. Golf gives the face a right to
left inclination. The automobile face, in its first stages, is one
in which fear that the thing will break down is mingled with
apprehension that there will be a collision at any moment.
There is a tremulous, uncertain pursing of the lips j and an
anxious look in the eyes. In the racing, or incurable stage,
there is little of the face left.
On the head Is a cap such as Dutch students â¢wear, horse
blinkers with glass at the eyeholes cover the upper part of the
countenance, and a close fitting hood encircles the ears. cheek3
and neck. For the very worst of the species there are nose
and mouth fenders, to make breathing possible when soing
sixty miles an hour.
THE AUTOMOBILE FACE. AS SHOWN IN THE ILLUSTRATION, IS BY NO MEANS A THING OF BEAUTY. IT
IS THE FACE ONE SEES WHEN THE CHAUFFEUR, GOING AT GREAT SPEED. FEARS A BREAKDOWN
OR A COLLISION. -

After recalling Hearst's statement that "bar one" McKinley "is the most despised
and hated creature in the hemisphere, his name is hooted at, his figure burned in effigy,"
the New York Sun says for the American people: "They remember and they do not for
give Mr. Hearst. All over the country there is a bitter feeling against him. It is use
less for him to put an onion to his eyes and boohoo. He now occupies the place which
he assigned to that gentle and generous statesman whom he loved to slander. The de
scription needs but one slight modification. 'Bar none' Hearst is now 'the most de
spised and hated creature in the hemisphere. His name is hooted at/his figure burned.'
And what an incalculable public misfortune it would be if this were not so."
The Jersey City News says: "The crime of Hearst is not the censure of McKin
ley. We believe the Jersey City News has spoken of the President on innumerable occa
sions in terms of severity. * * * The difference is that the New York Journal ap
peals to the knife, the pistol and the bomb for,remedy. Our appeal is to public opinion,
to public discussion and to the ballot. While we endeavor to rally the people in the
names of Jefferson, Jackson and Cleveland, the Hearst organs hold up Jack Cade, Marat
and Robespierre as heroes and exemplars for patriotism. These papers have repeatedly
dared glorify the excesses of the French Revolution and have, printed articles in direct,
open praise of assassination, with plain intimation that it was applicable to the evils of
our own da}'. No,} it will not do for Mr. Hearst to try to evade his responsibility. He
is a preacher of riot, loot and assassination. John Most has never gone further. Let
the people see him as he is and judge him and his papers."
The Perth Amboy Republican says: "Mr. Hearst deliberately makes his great
newspapers vehicles of falsehood, agents of destruction. He is like a quack who offers
some deadly opiate as a panacea for all namable diseases, well knowing that the drug
will paralyze the mind and the will and inevitably create in its victim a demand for more.
When Czolgosz comes to the electric chair let it. not be forgotten that the real culprit is
not the unbalanced, weak degenerate who held the pistol, but William R. Hearst, of
whose teachings the -act was the legitimate outcome."
Referring to Hearst's style of journalism the New Haven Leader says: "The de
mand for the suppression of this pernicious influence is universal. Relentlessly, inde
cently, outrageously, the yellow journals have denounced every man in public life and
have sought to increase circulation by the crudest and most indecent cartoons, supple
mented by red ink headlines, editorial vituperation and persistent, constant effort to ar
ray-mass against class and arouse the worst passions of mankind. The time has come
when this kind of journalism can no longer be safely tolerated, when the good name of
this nation demands that such debauchery of public principle be stopped and stopped
forever."
Dealing with the Examiner's reply to* the indignation of the people the Amador
Ledger says: "Its only.^defense is that it is 'the paper of the people.' What people? Its
columns show only too clearly the kind of people it^io the mouthpiece for â the Mosts,
Czolgoszes, and anarchists generally. Its villainous system of cartooning and vilification
has done more to weaken the respect for law and good government than the ravings of
a score of out and out anarchist . lecturers. And its' conduct at this crisisâ when the mal
edictions of the American people against journals of its stripe are being heard on every
hand â only deepen the public contempt." \ ,-\u25a0'â¢â¢
The Ukiah Republican.-Press says: "Looking tack over the history of the past
seven or eight years, the fact stands out prominently that the yellow newspapers have
been fanning the spirit of anarchy. Their business is to sell newspapers. by methods of
sensationalism and playing upon the basest passions of mankind. They receive as news a
simple fact and proceed to surround it with a mass of glaring fiction and startling sur
mises. They represent the idea that the Government and -social system are diseased to
the core." , ." ' . . 'V- -
The LOS Angeles Times says: "The widespread movement which has been begun
by the decent and sdf-respecting portion of the American people against the slimy and
traitorous sheets. published by that pervert and degenerate, W. R. Hearst, is a righteous
movement. It had its origin in the horror and reprobation which every/ American heart
feels because of the act of the assassin Czolgosz, and because of the vile and venomous
influences which have-been permitted to work their ends, culminating in the most atro
cious crime which has been committed in the history of the republic."
The Arcata Union in commenting upon the claim of the Examiner. that it is "an
American newspaper for the American people"' says: "That paper taught that McKin
ley was a menace to human liberty, and the pitiful assassin believed he was carrying out
the will of the people when he took his life. But now the insincerity, and dishonesty
of this yellow sheet- become apparent.'. Immediately after his death whole pages are
taken up in. praise of McKinley. He is placed- upon a pinnacle beside Washington and
Lincoln and lauded to the skies as the foremost statesman of trie age.. * * * The
'she fiend' Goldman, who incited the crime; is at least consistent and defies public opin
ion by expressing her, utter indifference to the dastardly act. Czolgosz glories in his act
and welcomes, death, believing that he carried out the teachings of Goldman and the
The MarySVille Appeal says: "The public has a short memory as a rule, and in
time the Examiner may win its way back to favor to ? an abbreviated extent. But it is
losing heavily all round just now, and iHs to be hoped that the good work will con
tinue until the 'Anarchearsts' have been taught such a lesson that they will never a^ain
fly in the face of public decency." / T
The New York Press says of Hearst's reply to his critics: "And so having put in
your defense 'human,' .animated,' 'entertainhig,' 'light word,' 'funny,' you are goin<* to
resume the. humanity; the animation, the entertainment, the lightness and the fun. For
you do not propose that the mere assassination of the President by one of your disciples
shall make of your merry journalistic life a gloomy graveyard. Assassins may people
our graveyards with public men, murderers may hasten our Presidents to the tomb an
archists may play at -bowls with the skulls of our chosen servants, but yellow journalism
will not cease its 'human' incendiarism, its 'animated' .license, its /entertaining' incitement
its 'light' crime breeding, its 'funny' anarchism. Now. that the funeral is over you will
resume. But you willjnpt. * * * You will not resume if "the American people re-
Such is the general tone of the comment with; which Hearst's defense published
in his three papers has been met. The voice of the press of the East and of the West
attests the fact that the condemnation of the yellow 'journals was not a mere spasm of
wrath which would pass away as Hearst supposed in a few days. On the contrary, that
indignation is the outcome of long years of disgust with Hearst journalism, but which
found no adequate expression for itself until .the assassination of- McKinley roused the
whole people to vehement denunciations of the common enemy of law, society, religion
and government. ' ";^ _"' \u25a0;. ':Â£Â£Â£ --^V ;.":.. ;f.; : : : '* ; .. - ; ' " / \u25a0 ' -

omously vilified and cartooned President McKinley are . now seeking to escape public

him, and have really incited his murder. To arrest a contemptible cur like Johann Most,
a New York anarchistic editor, and allow immunity to William R. Hearst, the proprie
tor of the New York Journal, Chicago American and San Francisco Examiner/ is an ab- 1
surdrty. The assassination of the President is one of the direct fruits of .the anarchistic
teachings of Hearst's vile trjo of yellows."
. ThejChicago Journal says: "That some great evil or calamity must follow the in
cendiary journalism ; of W. R. Hearst was always obvious.. What would be the character
of the blow, or upon whom it would fall, could only be a matter of conjecture, and no one
even dreamed that the late President who so clearly possessed the affections of the
people could become a victim. And yet in the retrospect of the course Mr. Hearst has
pursued with his three papers it now seems logical enough that President McKinley
should be the bright and shining mark for hatred and discontent, for malice and'anarchy
W Y EARST'S defense of himself has now -been' before" the American people long
I I enough for its effect upon public sentiment to be noted. In no quarter has it
1 I "produced. any change in the tone of popular sentiment except to strengthen in-;
"*\u25a0 \u25a0"\u25a0 dignation against his lying journals and increase public disgust .with his shame
less effrontery and hypocrisy. Ample evidence to that effect is shown in the, continued
utterances, of the press of all sections of the Union denouncing yellow journalism and,
its "crocodile tears/'-

MOSQUITOESâ H." T. M., Crystal
Springs. Cal. The larvae of. the common
mosquito are bred from eggs laid in stag
nant water and can .always be differen
tiated from other water insects by the
peculiar" habit of rising to the surface tail
first and floating there, darting toward
the bottom at any. disturbance. They
come to the surface for air. the breathing
apparatus being in the tail. Any pond,
however stagnant, can be kept free from
these pests by keeping" the surface cov
ered with oil. A very small quantity
poured on the water -will soon diffuse
itself over the entire area and in. a very
short time will kill. every "wiggler" in
the pond. The oil clogs the air holes of
the wigglers and they die in convulsions.
\ UNMARRIED WOMAN'S RIGHTSâ
Settler, Susanville, Cal. The act of Con
gress approved June 6, 1900. provides that:
; "Where an ' unmarried woman who has
heretofore settled, or may hereafter set
tle, upon a tract of public land, has im
proved, established or maintained a bona
fide residence thereof, with the intention
of appropriating the same for a home,
subject to the homestead law, and has
married, or shall hefeafter marry, before
making entry of said land.- or before
making application to enter said land.
she shall not, on account of her marriage,
forfeit her right. to make entry and re
ceive patent for the land; provided, that
she does not abandon her residence on
said land, and is otherwise qualified to
make homestead entry; provided further,
that the man whom she marries is riot at
the tim6 of their marriage claiming a sep
arate .tract of land under: the. homestead
law "' '" '
Just now the cut of men's clothes so
much in vogue because It is intended to
give a man with any sort of shape, or
even no shape at all, a military, athletic
looking appearance has introduced wifh
it a new way of. greeting your male
friends. It is a sort of modification of
the military officer's salute, and Is very
easy to learn and makes a distinguished
and graceful, salute, i
It is also the very latest, and so, if you
wear your clothes cut In the latest
fashion, you of course must not neglect
learning it. ' .'

THE following deadly editorial appeared in
Hearst's New York Evening Journal on
May 22, 1 901 , less than six months be-
fore President McKinley was shot down
by a pupil of yellow, journalism:
"What is an anarchist?"
"According to accepted definitions, issued
by the higher classes of society, an anarch-
ist is one who tries to do something useful
that has never been done before.
"There is a change coming some day,
whether the gentlemen who denounce char-
ity as anarchy want the change or not."
WHAT IS AN ANARCHIST?

iner please teil what it meant when on
Februarys 1900s it published, from its
Washington correspondent, the following:
"The bullet that pierced Goebei's chest
Cannot be found in all the West;
Good reason. It is speeding here
To stretch McKinley on his bier."

"Water Front Notes.
The Panama Rallroad^Company's char
tered steamer Enterprise 'Sfrived from the
isthmus yesterday. Captain Miller was
sick during part of the voyage, but Is
now as well as ever. His wife and
daughter accompanied him on the run.
The Enterprise was purchased for the
Hilo trade, but was chartered to carry a
load of barley to Panama. She may be
sent down again with another . cargo of
grain. She brought up a full cargo pf New
York merchandise and a number of steer
age passengers.

The records of this board will disclose that
Professor Lyser has repeatedly preferred
charges asrainst some of the oldest and most
experienced, teachers In the department, which
upon trial have not been sustained.
George O. Mitchell, the teacher in the
Girls' High School, who was cited to ex
plain.why he had not paid a $300 tailor bill,
appeared and promised the board that he
would settle the bill at once. He thus
escapes suspension under the rule impos
ing that penalty on teachers who do not
pay their just debts. \u25a0'
Attorney S. V. Costello filed a petition
signed by the parents"of all the pupils in
the John Swett School under the tuition
of Miss Mary C. McKinney, against whom
charges have been preferred by Principal
Albert Lyser. The parents certify that
they are entirely satisfied with the
progress made by their children and re
quest that Miss McKinney be retained in
her position.
In answer to Lyser' s charge that the
mind of Miss McKinney is in such an ab
normal condition as to make it impossible
for her to' sustain amicable relations with
the other teachers, Costello says that Mrs.
J. H. Erikson is the only teacher with
whom Miss McKinney is not friendly.
He adds that there are eighteen teachers
in the school. Coatello pays:
The first appointments from the new
eligible list of teachers were made yester
day by the Board of Education, which
elected Miss Elizabeth B. Moore and Miss
Cecilia H. Gilchrist to succeed Miss Helen
Crocker of the Reddin? and Miss Elinor
Murphy of the John Swett School, both
of whom resigned. The first named two
stand first and second respective'ly on the
list.

'news of the ocean.
There were two deaths during the voy
age from Manila and one during the run
down. On September 24 Virgil D. Moss of
Company G, Twenty-second Infantry,
died and A. Muller of Company B,
Twenty-fifth Infantry, passed away the
next day. On \u25a0 the way down Walter
Brinkley, a waiter, died of peritonitis.
Generals Sternberg and Weston and Con
gressmen Kahn and Shafroth have been
on a tour of inspection in" the Philip
pines. All of them are in the best of
health and are jubilant over tho condition
and extent of Uncle Sam's new posses
sions.

THOMAS MADE SLOW TIME.
Among the passengers on the Han
cock was Miguel de Gamo. a Filipino, who
came here as a stowaway. As he had no
money and no means of support the Gov
ernment sent him back home, much
ap-ainst his will.
One of the transport's crew was Robert
Arthur Maeondray. He shipped as a deck
hand a few hours before the vessel sailed
and is now qualifying as an A. B. It is
only a year ago since young Maeondray
was married to Miss Madge Richardson
in this city. Their honeymoon was spent
in Japan and the young couple had a
splendid time. Four months ago Mrs.
Maeondray sued for and obtained a di
vorce on the ground of cruelty. Love's
youns dream was over and hard work
was the only thing left for the husband.
He decided that his vocation was the sea
so he shipped on the Hancock as a deck-

NEW YORK. Oct. 1.â The lively interest in
to-day's International yacht race took many
Stock Exchange members away from the board
room and still further diminished the volume
of trading. The day's aggregate sales were
barely 300,000 shares, and the fluctuation of
prices was .very narrow for the most part:
The exceptions worthy of special note were
in the industrial list and among the dormant
and less important railroad stocks. Liquida
tion in Sugar was the feature of the market,
following a sharp break in prices yesterday.
The usual lack of information regarding the
movement was one of its features. The room
traders joined in the selling in an attempt to
make a quick return -and their covering
caused a rally of a point. The stock's ex
treme decline was 4>,i. The gossip to account
lor the decline included rumors that the in
siders were liquidating on apprehension of a
change in the sugar tariH at the coming- ses
sion of Congress or of unfriendly, legislation.
Other rumors had it that Insiders who had
sold at recent high prices were depressing
the price to get back their stock. The gen
eral ieeling or distrust of the blind pool meth
ods of the great industrial corporations, which
liad reached an acute stage since the recent
episode In Amalgamated Copper, was a factor
In Sugar and in a number of other Industrials
There were declines of lVi@2 points in Brook
lyn Transit. Colorado Fuel, National Salt pre
ferred. General Electric and American Ex
presa. The waiting attitude of the market
was partly due to expectations of dividend
action on the United .States Steel stocks and
the presentation of a promised financial state
ment. The non-appearance of this as the' 'ses
sion drew toward its close emphasized the
weakness of the industrials. Amalgamated
Copper was rather firm, rising a point, then
losing It and then stiffening to the bÂ»>st
Aichison was quite notably firm and gained
a point on rumors that the directors would
increase the semi-annual dividend rate to 2
per cent at a meeting to-morrow. There were
other Hrm spots in the railroad list and the
funeral undertone was rather firm on a be
lated recognition of yesterdays important re
cuperation of the money market resources bv
receipts of Australian and French gold The
aCt !Â° n of the market was hesitating and un
certain throughout.
The railroad bond market was dull and !-
regular. Total sales, $l,400,CC0
United States refunding 2s advanced W per
cent on the last call.

The Weather and Crops.
The repcrt of A. G. McAdie, section director
of the climate and crop service of the weather
bureau, for the week ending Monday is as
follows:
GENERAL SUMMARY.
The temperature has been considerably be
low normal in all sections during the week,
but the weather has been generally pleasant
and favorable for fruit drying and late crops.
Light rain has fallen throughout the State,
and there v.as quite a heavy fall of snow in
the mountains east of Fresno on the 23d.
Grain thrashing and hay bailing are still
progressing in many places. Reports received
at this office do not show any considerable
damage to grain and hay by the rain of last
\u25a0vveck, although exposed grain in sacks and
unbaled hay in some sections were slightly
Injured. There are no reports of serious dam
age to beans and hops. Bean harvest Is pro
gressing favorably, with good results thus far.
Hops are all gathered and baling is nearly
completed; the yield and quality are about
average. Sugar beets are yielding more than
an average crop in some sections. Late veg
etables and pasturage were greatly benefited
by the rains.
Raisin making and fruit drying are pro
gressing rapidly, though temporarily delayed
by the rain on Tunday. As ample warnings
had been given, fruit trays were generally
stacked and but little damage resulted from
the rain. In some of the northern vineyards
there was some damage to grapes, which had
commenced cracking badly. Walnut picking
bad commenced. Citrus fruits are In excel
lent condition.
SACRAMENTO VALLEY. â Cool, clear
weather continued after the rain of last Mon
day, followed by light showers Sunday. The
cool weather was bc-neficial to grapes, which
have not, fo far as reports indicate, been
seriously injured by the rains. There are
no reports of damage to grain and hay. and
it is not probable that the light ra!n of yes
terday injured these crops. Pasturage and
late vegetables were considerably benefited by
the rains, and in pome places the soil was
Foftened sufficiently for seeding summer-fal
low. Hops are all fathered and baling is
nearly complttfd in Yuba County. Oranges
are in excellent condition. The Sacramento
River rose one foot at Hed Bluff as a result
cf the ram last Monday.
COAST AND BAY SECTIONS.-The rain of
last week caused considerable damage to
grapes, grain and hay in some of the north
ern counties, but crops were not materlally
injured in the central and southern <*>unties
At Peachlar.d the rainfall up to the ">6ih wai :
1.2S Ir.ches, and grapes were cracking badlv
Dried fruits and ralsir.? were mostly under
cover before the rain. GraiÂ«? picking is pro
presEin*: rapidly. Exceptionally fine apules
are being marketed in Humboldt County and
the yield Is abundant in all the northern dis
tricts. Prunes and crapes are light crops in
the vicinity of Califtoga, but prunes are of
large size. Hop picking is completed in So
noma County. Beans and susar beets are
yielding eood crops. Gr^Jn thrashing and hav
baling will not be completed for several week's
in San Benlto County.
SAN JOAQUIN VALLEY.â Light rain fell
penerally over the valley Sunday night and
Monday, but the danuge to drying fruit was
very light. As ample warnings had been
Kiven nearly all of the raisins on the trays
had been stacked and the grain was in the
varehouses. " The remaining portion of the
we^k was cool and somewhat unsettled, preatly
retarding raisin drying. A large portion of
the flrst crop is ready for the sweat boxes.
A gTeat many grapes are going to the wineries.
Teach and prune orops are about all har
vested. The sweet potato crop is being- har
vested. Imports from the mountain region
east of Fresno show that three or four inches
of snow fell on Monday, which retarded lum
bering. In 6ome portions of the valley plow
ing and seeding grain are progressing. Feed
anl water are plentiful and stock. of all kinds
are dclnff well.
SOUTHERN CALIFORNIA.â Cooler weather
has prevailed during the v.eek, with heavy
fops along the coast Grain thrashing and
hay baling are not yet completed, but pro
gressing; rapidly. The hay crop is above aver
age in some sections. Barley is heavy, but
of poor quality. Bean harvest is progressing.
The sugar beet crcp is heavier than usual.
Corn le about average. There is a heavy crop
of tomatoes, excellent in quality. Potatoes
are looking well. Walnut picking is progress
ins. Citrus fruits are In excellent condition
LOS ANGELES SUMMARY.â Cool, foggy
weather continues. Fruit drying is retarded;
conÂ«j damage. Almond harvest continues ;
fomewhat delayed. Favorable reports of citrus
crop. Growing fruits and vegetables are fair.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL,
FORECAST.
The pressure has risen rapidly during the
past twenty-four hours over Nevada and North
ern California. Unsettled weather prevails
generally over Eastern California and the
Sierras. A thunderstorm is reported at Inde
pendence and high northwest winds are re
ported in the Nevada desert. The temperature
has risen about 16 degrees at Sacramento and
has fallen rapidly over Nevada. In the great
valleys of California the temperatures are
slightly below the normal, and conditions are
unfavorable for raisin making and fruit drying, i
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty I
hours ending midnight October 2: j
Northern California â Cloudy Wednesday, with
light clearing up showers in the foothills; light
northerly winds.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday except
showers in the foothills; light westerly winds.
Nevadaâ Showers Wednesday, warmer; high
northwest winds.
San Francisco and vicinityâ Cloudy Wednes
day, fresh southerly changing to brisk wes
terly winds.
ALEXANDER G. McADIE,
Forecast Official.

Deciduous and Citrus Fruits
The market continues quiet, as usual at this
time of the- year. The Weather Bureau reports
Grapes cracking in several sections, owing to
the rains. Table Grapes are more or less slow,
.but Wine Grapes are very firm everywhere.
Limes are firm at the recently improved
prices. Lemons are in larger supply and lower,
but Oranges are In lighter supply and firm.
Choice Bananas are scarce, and even Inferior
stock Is not plentiful.
The summer fruits, such as Peaches. Plums
and Figs, show no new features. They are al
most always featureless at the close of the
season. Melons are more or less dull, owing
to the cool and showery weather.
A car of Cape Cod Cranberries came In. but
had hjcen all sold out prior to arrival.

Dried Fruits, Nuts and Raisins.
Prices for most fruits are easier, though the
demand continues very fair, and the output
of the State is moving out well. A very good
trade In Nuts Is reported, but Honey is quiet.
Raisins are very unsettled owing to the differ
ent cliques that are trying to control the crop.
The demand for Prunes has not yet fully
! opened, though a fair Inquiry for shipment is
reported.

WHEATâ Paris futures were lower. Liver
pool remained about the same.
Chicago was listless, with a poor demand,
and everybody short. The bears raided the
market, but prices yielded stubbornly. St. Louis
exporters reported a better demand from the
Continent. The Kansas City Star reported
that the great corn counties in the northern
part of the State were planting thousands of
acres of corn land to wheat, and that it would
not be surprising if the wheat crop of that
section equals the corn crop next year. There
will be about 6,000,000 acres of each.
Bradstreet's weekly estimate gave stocks an
Increase of 3.44S.0CO bushels, as follows: East
of the Rockies, 3,049,000 bushels; Europe and
afloat, 300,000.
This market continued I dull, with a further
shading in futures.

CHICAGO, Oct. 1.â The last hour of the
wheat market, brought the bear contingent
out in force and with little effort they suc
ceeded in hammering the prices severely. De
cember opened %@V4c lower at 70%c to 70@
70%c and sagged a bit lower during the suc
ceeding hour. Covering brought December
back to 70%@70%c later, but the bears, seizing
upon the liberality of receipts as a pretext,
began selling and although their sales were
not large the market was so dull that the
effect on the price was marked. December
dropped to egHigcgUc and closed lc lower at
69%c, with no recuperative power left, to all
appearances.
Lacking any influential trading corn was
inclined to follow wheat. December closed
weak, %@%c lower, at 56%@56Uc. \
Oats had a short-lived activity about the
middle of . the session when weak longs be- j
came scared at liberal offerings by a prom
inent operator. December closed weak, Yi'S%c
down at>34%c.
Provisions were easier all around en a
weaker hog market and liquidation. The out
side demand was slow and January pork
closed 12%c lower, lard a shade lower and
ribs 5c lower.
