London Wool Sales.
LONDON, Oct. 1.â The offerings at the wool
auction sales to-day amounted to 14,750 bales,
principally medium. Fine grades were in good
demand, but medium and coarse were irregu
lar and In buyers' favor. A slight decline In
prices brought about free buying in the lower
grades. Cross-breds were in fair supply and
met with a steady demand.

London Market.
NEW YORK, Oct. 1.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's London financial cablegram says:
Th3 stock market to-day was Idle in the ex
treme, but closed cheerful on a rally in the
Copper stocks. Rio Tintos opened at 46,
dropped to 43 9-16 and then sharply rallied.
The dividend, due on Thursday, is expected
to be thirty shillings, against 'forty shillings.
Probably 35s could be paid, but the board of
directors Is quite likely to prefer to augment
the reserves.-- Anacondas opened at 7Â«4 and

quotations. Nothing was done in the \u25a0 street
sava in United States Steel, which is still

per stocks is 273 tons, in supplies 73 tons.

by the Indians.

Licensed to Marry.
O. B. Hewitt and F. A. Plymire, who
were arrested last August for practicing
dentistry 'without a license and who
sought to evade serving a term in prison
by securing a writ of habeas corpus, were
remanded Into custody yesterday by Judge
Seawell.
Hewitt, who was convicted on a charge
preferred by Percy T. Bowyer, instituted
habeas corpus proceedings, claiming thai
the act regulating dental surgery Is un
constitutional and that the Police Judge
before whom he was tried acted without
proper jurisdiction.
Judge Seawell In rendering the decision
said that the matter should be passed,
upon by the Supreme Court, as the dis
puted measure had received the votes of
both houses of the Legislature.
CONVICTED DENTISTS
REMANDED INTO CUSTODY
The Institute's drawing classrooms are
to be fitted up with new desks and other
appliances designed to make them per
fectly suitable to the needs of the pupils.
This evening the members of the insti
tute will hold a special meeting to deter
mine the matter of selling the Pavllioa
block to the city for the Public Library
site.
At a meeting of the board of trustees
of the Mechanics' Institute last night
Herbert Bashford was elected library as
sistant, vice J. A. Rowell, resigned. Miss
SI F. Wiliams, the cataloguer, was given
a four months' leave of absence on ac
count of sickness. -â¢
Librarian F. J. Tes&art reported the
daily average circulation of books last
month at 743, the highest average to date.
On September 21 there were 463 books Is
sued, the largest single day's business In
the history of the institute.
WILL CONSIDER SATYR
OF THE PAVIIilON SITB
"His opposition dates back to the time
or the Democratic convention three years
ago, when Dr. Clinton's name came up
for renomination. I was then the secre
tary of Mayor Phelan. Father Yorke
demanded the defeat of Clinton for re
nomination as Supervisor as Phelan had
the convention well under control. The
Mayor refused as Dr. Clinton had been
a good Supervisor and he thought it was
due both to the man and the party to
allow things to take their regular and
Just course. Clinton was renominated.
The Mayor did not Interfere. I know-
Father Yorke wanted me to use my In
fluence to have Clinton defeated for re
nomination.
"At Father Yorke's house, after the
election, Mayor Phelan and myself were
paying a call and the Clinton matter
came up. Father Yorke was very frigid
and the visit was not marked by its
cordiality."
"No good reason exists for the attack
Father Yorko has made upon me. Our
first relations began when I lent my sup
port to the organisation of the League
of the Cross Cadets in December, 1SD3.
A. " B.^ Magulre and Thomas H. Fallon
were the prime movers in the reorganiza
tion of the cadets. The organization was
changed at that time from a purely tem
perance society to a quasi military body.
Father Yorke gave us his support and
assistance In many ways. That Is what
he evidently means when he says ho
came to my assistance, for I was heart
and soul in the work at that time.
Chief of Police Sullivan, who has been
singled out for attack by Father Yorke.
exposes the reasons for the clergyman's
acrimony in an Interview. The bitter
ness of the prelate Is charged to political
disappointment at the time when Dr.
Charles A. Clinton was a candidate for
renomination as Supervisor.

lows:
"What I said about Father Torke be

Mrs. Cornelia WontworthJ a divorced
woman 35 ye.ars old, residing at 1915 Geary
street, committed suicide last Monday
night by inhaling illuminating gas. When
her 17-year-old son Lindley Apple left the
houee for the purpose of attending night
school she affixed one end of a piece of
rubber tubing to a gas fixture, turned the
key, 'lay down on the bed, placed the
other end of the tubing in her mouth and
thus ended her unhappy life. .
\u25a0 Her son returned home . from, school
shortly after 9 o'clock and found her dead
He raised an alarm and a physician was
hurriedly summoned, but life was extinct.
Mrs. Wentworth was divorced several
years ago from a man named Apple and
resumed her maiden name, Wentwbrth
Since the divorce she has been very low
spirited, and her son says that she twice
before attempted suicide, the first time by
means of laudanum and the second time
by the inhalation of illuminating gas.

The prosecution completed its testimony
before Judge Cabaniss yesterday after
noon in the case of T. H. Pagedon an-1
F. M. Cliff, non-union machinists, charged
with assault to murder for shooting Wil
liam Dunn, a striker, on the evening of
July 17. The defendants were working In
the Dundon Iron Works and were on
their way home \u25a0 when attacked by a
crowd of strikers. They fired to Brare
the crowd and one of the shots took effect
on Dunn. A motion to dismis3 was made
yesterday after the testimony was In, but
the Judge said he would deny it pro
forma, intimating that if the defendants
could give any reasonable explanation on
Saturday he would dismiss the ca3e.
Harry Howell, striking machinist, who
was arrested Monday morning for steal
ing an extension basket from a second
hand store on Mission street, near Fourth,
because he wanted money to buy food,
was convicted by Judge Conlan yester
day. He waived time for sentence anfi,
the Judge gave him three months in the
County Jail. .
George Jorgensen, a striking sailor liv
ing at 418 First street, appeared before
Judge Fritz yesterday on a charge of
petty larceny. He was attempting to
steal a piece of lumber from a yard at
the foot of Third street Monday, when
arrested by a special officer. He told the
Judge that he needed the wood, as he
has a family of four children to support,
and there was nothing in the house to
make a fire for cooking their food. He
thought it was Just an old piece of wood.
The Judge dismissed the case, but repri
manded Jorgensen, and pointed out to him
the result of a, conviction for petty lar-
Ce john Desmond, a striking teamster, sur
rendered himself to the police yesterday,
as he heard he was wanted on a charge
of assault with intent to murder. The
complaining witness is Tony Alborelli,
who was assaulted by a gang of strikers
recently and had his arm broken.' Des
mond was accused of being the leader of
the gang. He was at once released on'
$1000 bonds: Other strikers are wanted
for the same offense. \u25a0; ;; ; *;,:*&

Julius Kruttschnitt was busy yesterday
receiving the congratulations of his
friends. He accepted the congratulations
modestly and went about his business
with the quiet yet determined manner
that has distinguished him in the past.
He stated In an Interview that he will
devote part of his time to the operation
of the road and the balance to executive
business. He will still retain his olllces
in the Union Trust building, but at cer
tain hours of the day he will use the
executive offices.
The first official act of Julius Krutt
schnitt in the administration of Southern
Pacific affairs was to appoint R. 'H.
Ingram, formerly secretary to Charles M.
Hays, executive secretary. This appoint
ment proved a pleasant surprise to
Ingram s friends, who thought that under
the new regime he might return to the
Kast.

Clodomir and Juan Castillo, the Colom
bian Liberal leaders, appear to be at
Trier.ta, on the Colombian side of Goa
jira. with followers estimated to number
from 500 to 2000 men. It is rumored that
the forces under Castillo and Davilaa*
men, if rescued from their predicament.
Numberless rumors are being indus
triously circulated, but a careful weigh
ing of all information from various
sources leads to the belief that the forces
of Davila, with five companions, succeeded
in reaching Fort San Carlos, thirty miles
north of Maracaibo, a Venezuelan base
and political prison, whence he asked that
reinforcements be sent to rescue his com
mand, reported to be in distress and in
danger from the Indians inhabiting Goa
jira,, who, under the leadership of their
cacique, Juan Dolores, are fighting on the
side of the Colombian Government.

A leaflet sent to the legation by the Co
lombian Consul at Curacao gives details
of the. recent fight at Curasua, about fif
teen miles from Rlo.Hacha, on the Goa
jira Peninsula, and which probably is
identical with that referred to by Minis
ter Mendez in his dispatch to Dr. Silva
to-day. It shows that the victory over
the enemy was complete, the Colomb'ans
taking four batteries of artillery. Gatlin^
guns and a considerable amount of am
munition. General Echeverria, who led
a portion of the expedition, the represen
tative says, was killed, contrary to pre
vious advices, which said he had fallen
captive into the hands of the Colombians.
. WILLEMSTAD, Island of Curacao, Oct.
1, via Haitien cable.â Advices received
here by mall from Maracaibo under date
of September 2S says that Venezuela is
protecting the Goajira frontier by rein
forcements. An expedition composed of
about 1500 recruits from Lavela de Coro;
on the- Gulf of Coro and Merida, with
Vincente Sanchea in command, left here
to-day for some point near Goajira, whence
they will probably move Inland toward
the frontier. The foregoing covers the
facts in the case.
Some uncertainty exists in the minds cf
the officials of the legation as to whether
this victory over the Venezuelans Is iden
tical -with, that victory heretofore re
ported via Curacao, in the press dis
patches, or is the result of another fight.
Dr. Silva, the Minister, is Inclined to the
former opinion. Some stress is laid on
the statement that this invasion was
without a previous declaration of war on
Venezuela's part. This, Dr. Silva says,
shows the irregularity of the course the
latter country has taken. . Dr. Mendez,
who signs the dispatch. Is the new Min
ister of Foreign Affairs, whose appoint
ment followed the Colombian Cabinet
crisis reported from Bogota yesterday.

During the day it was developed that
the telegram from General Otis relating
to sites for the hemp factory at Manila
was addressed to the Adjutant General. A
letter from Acting Secretary Sanger said
there was no record of a dispatch to Gen
eral Otis which called for the reply.
Major Hawkes told of a conversation
with General Corbin in relation to a set
tlement with Heistand for his services in
connection with the hemp company. He
said General Corbin told him the bill
ought to be paid and to make out his bill
and he (Corbin) would forward it through
military channels. General Corbin had
informed him . emphatically that ho was
not in the company and had nothing to do
with it. Hawkes said -he made out the
bill, but Corbin never forwarded it.
He also said that in an interview with
Assistant Secretary Allen the latter de
nied emphatically that he had any con
nection with the company. Hawkes said
he had numerous interviews with Assist
ant Secretary Meiklejohn, to whom he
had presented his claim through the
mails. Mr. Meiklejohn had said Heistand
ought to pay it. Mr. Meiklejohn, he said,
also appointed him in the customs service
In the Philippines and also promised him
promotion. Mr. Meiklejohn asked him for
recommendations before making the ap
pointment.
-Major Hawkes gave the details of his
trip to the Philippines and his return after
his appointment was revoked. He saw
Secretary Root, but the committee would
not permit him to tell anything of. what
passed between them, holding that Secre
tary Root was | not involved in the case.
Hawkes said he had interviews with Mr.
Meiklejohn In which - the latter told him
that it would be impossible for him to se
cure^ a position under the Government so
long as Mr. Root was Secretary of War.

A picturesque march, followed by equal
ly picturesque tableaux, was the leading
feature of â¢ the entertainment given in
the Native Sons' Hall last evening in the
Carnival of Ancient and Modern Arts.
When the festivities were at their height
the hall was a scene of remarkable bril
liancy. The costumes of many nations
were displayed by those who are taking
part In the carnival. The booths, attend
ed by handsomely gowned ladies and
stocked with articles of beauty and use,
were even handsomer than on the open
ing night. A large audience listened to
the music furnished by the band of the
Hebrew Orphan Asylum and enjoyed the
tableaux. In the grand march Uncle Sam
and Columbia were attended by Johnny
Bull, who was impersonated by Mr. Mur
ray.
It was English night, and the tableaux
were "Columbia at the Court of Spain,"
\u25a0which was presented by the ladles of the
Brozik booth, Mrs. R. E. White presi
dent; "Scene From the Mikado," by the
ladies of the Sir Arthur Sullivan booth,
Mrs. J. M. Dixon president, and a scene
from "The Termagant" by the ladles of
Mrs. Chester Keough's booth. The ladles
were assisted by gentlemen who volun
teered their services.
This evening the French will celebrate
and Mrs. M. A. Cachot, Mrs. Francis
Hunt and Mrs. Van der Nailen will have
charge of the tableaux.

A tpam belonging to McNab & Smith
became stalled at Sixth and Harrison
streets yesterday morning and a large
crowd of strike sympathizers gathered,
hooting at the driver and special. A
report reached the Hall of Justice that
twj> men had been killed by strikers dur
ing this trouble and a general warning
was sent out to draymen not to allow
any more teams to be driven over the
route where the trouble was in progress.
A squad of police was hurried to the
scene and soon succeeded in dispersing
the mob. No attempts at violence were
made during the demonstration.
a driver for Teele & Co. went yester
day morning to the Southern Pacific
freight house for a load of goods. While
he was Inside getting his merchandise
somp one removed a nut from one of the
wheels of the wagon. He discovered
what had been done before he attempted
to drive away and in company with an
officer traced a group of men who were
seen near the wagon to the teamstera'
headquarters, where the missing nut was
found. Everybody at the headquarters
denied having removed the nut and would,
give no explanation as to how it came
there.

THE marriage of Julius Ach and
Misa Beatrice Sachs will take
place at the residence \u25a0 of the
bride's parents, 1904 Franklin
street, on next Tuesday evening
at 6 o'clock. The nuptial knot will be tied
by Rabbi. Voorsanger, the contracting
parties standing under a huge floral can
opy in which will be suspended a wed
ding bell of white buds. , The interior of
the â¢ residence will be beautifully deco
rated for the occasion. ,
The groom will have Arthur SUverberg
for his best man, but the bride will have
no -attendants except Abe Grossmayer,
her uncle, and Walter Sachs, her brother,
who will be the ribbon-bearers and also
assist in receiving, the wedding guests.
The bride will be given away by her
father, and orchestral music will lend
gayety to the pleasajit affair.
Several hundred guests will be present
at the ceremonyand at the wedding sup
per that is to follow in the huge banquet
room of the residence. The couple will
take an extended Eastern and possibly
European . trip for their honeymoon. On
musical event that has yet taken
place in California is already shown in
many ways. Among the more notable
artists to appear will be Edouard de
Reszke, the eminent basso, whose bril
liant success of last season is still fresh
In the public memory. M. de Reszke,
who Is in superb voice, will be a con
spicuous feature of the coming engage
ment, snd will be heard In several new
roles. By cablegram the complete roster

Train Goes Through Bridge in Mex
ico and Two of Crew Are
Killed.
AUSTIN, Tex., Oct. 1.â A dispatch from
Monterey, Mexico, says a freight train on
the Monterey and Mexican Gulf Railroad
went through a bridge near Lomo Alto.
Among the cars was one loaded with dy
namite. The concussion caused it to ex
plode, adding horror to the destructive
ness of the wreck. Two members of the
train crew were killed.

the present strike was reported yesterday
to M. J. White, secretary of the Soclety
for the Prevention of Cruelty to Children.
The case was that of the family of Ed
ward Campbell, longshoreman, and
member of the Longshoremen's Union, re
siding at 141 Main street.
Secretary White on investigation found
the mother and four little children living
in a wretched hovel in absolute want.
There was nothing in the house for the
mother and children to eat and not a cent
of money with which to buy anything.
Besides bein? in danger of starvation, the
wretched family was also in danger of
I'-'Fing Its home. Since the strike com
menced they have had no money with
which to pry the rent and last week the
'fwncr of the house had the furniture
thrown into the street. But after the
landlord learned how destitute the family
was he gave permission for them to oc
cupy the house for another week.
Before the strike was declared Edward
Campbell as a. longshoreman was earning
FufScier.t money to support his famllv, but
as :-oon as the strike wÂ».s commenced and
he wa.Â« forced into idleness his family was
quickly reduced to suffering and want.
Campbell, as a member of the Longshore
men's Union, waf? entitled to strike
benefits, but his benefits amounted only
to a email suppiy of groceries. And the
supplies of groceries, insufficient as they
were for a growing tamilv. were discon

FAMILY IS STARVING.
members of the Metal Trades Association
ure determined more than ever not: to
accede to the demand of the strikers for
fa. nine hour day. Still, they fail to under
stand why the union permitted so many
of its men to return to work on an agree
ment calling lor ten hours temporarily,
and immecuateiy thereafter recalled the
machinists, all of whom were willing to
continue operations on the ten hour basis.
The only tneory advanced for this by the
metal trades men is that the union otiicers
feared that the moral effect upon the
strikers when it was gv neraliy known that
ten hours was the rule at the National
Foundry would be to cause a general
stampede, regardless of official ar
rangements, in order that the ieaders
mignt not lose control of the men, pre
sumably, it was decided to hold out for
the nine hour rule, and for that reason, it
Is argued, the union men at the National
Iron Works were recalled.
The statement issued by the Machinists'
Union at its meeting- Monday night that
the men returned to the National Iron
Works without any definite arrangement
concerning hours having been reached is
declared to be absurd by Messrs. Mar-
Ehutz and CantrelL proprietors of that
foundry. They a#ert that the men re
turned under the positive agreement that
they were to work ten hours until the
regular winter schedule of nine hours was
inaugurated. After the leaders had ac
cepted this proposition, the employers
reason, they realized that the act would
be regarded by the public as virtual
defeat for the strikers, and they therefore
recalled the men as stated.
The National Foundry was virtually
closed yesterday. In anticipation of the
possible return of the men who worked
Monday, the fires were started, but none
of the machinists appeared. A dozen or
two pickets were on hand, however. In
readiness to "argue"' the question with
the men should they come, but their
watching was fruitless. Several of the
men showed up during the day and had a
conference with Mr. Cantre'll, but the
latter stated that it was impossible to
grant the demand made upon him. This
was accepted a? final and the men re
turned to their homes.
That the machinists do not agree with
their leaders in all particulars was evi
denced when two union men went to worn
at the Union Iron Works. In addition to
these te:i molders and helpers were added
to the working force at that institution.
The management of the Risdon Iron
Works say that the ten men secured on
Monday worked yesterday and that they
were satisfied with the situation. The
men themselves say that they will con
tinue io work as long: as they are wanted.
They assert that they need money and
that the ten hour scale is perfectlysatis
factory to them.
The failure of the National Iron Works
people to retain the twenty-two men who
returned to work on Monday was the
subject of general comment among the
metal trades yesterday. It was believed
that the return cf the union machinists
In accordance with an agreement to work
ten hours practically settled the machin
ists' strike. When, after working half a
day, the men decided to work nine hours
only and promptly quit work at the expir
ation of that time the situation assumed
a new and more disagreeable phase.

operations at the National Foundry, the
A meeting of hog owners has been called
for next Saturday at the Courthouse in
this city and steps will be taken to have
State and Government experts visit this
county and study the disease, which has
already caused a loss of upward of $50,000.
R. H. Blossom has lost 1000 hogs, Charles
Hesse 200, M. Gonsalves 223, Frank
Hougton . 250, W. Lantz., 500 and many
others smaller numbers. The disease has
just reached J. W. Boyd's band and J.
I. Casale has noticed' symptoms in his
drove of 500. It is estimated that 4000
hogs have died from the disease in this
county.
RED BLUFF, Oct. 1.â There has been
great mortality among hogs' in Tehama
County and in one instance, out of a drove
of 540, 500 have died. It is a fact worthy
of note that the hogs in good condition
withstand the disease much better than
those in poorer .condition and young hogs
suffer first.

''But that the American press is improv
ing and Is bound to improve cannot, I
think, be doubted by any one who has
come in .contact, as I have done, with the
men who have made it. However much
one may differ from their standpoint or
deprecate their methods, it is impossible
to deny their quick intelligence and
breadth of view, or the thorough earnest
ness and uprightness of purpose by which
the conductors of the press are actuated,
while In that which makes a press greater
in independence ( and incorruptibility,
financial, political or social, the American
can compare on equal terms with the
English press."

to-day by a special correspondent who
was recently in America, which is- cor
rectly attributed to Moberly Bell, man
ager of the Times, describes his experi
ences with interviewers and their capacity
to provide interesting copy, whether the
person interviewed contributes thereto or
not. In conclusion, however, the corre
spondent pays a high tribute to the
American Dress, as follows:
He also asked that some money that
was due him from the Montana Central
Railroad be sent to a niece, and ttyen he
was pulled up. The end of the rope was
tied to a pole and the crowd dispersed.
Later Sheriff McConnell cut the body
down and placed it In a coffin. \u25a0 There
will be an investigation to-day.
The march to the hanging place was
quiet. Brady, was given a chance to say
a word. He declared that they had the
wrong man, although he had been posi
tively identified by his victim and a score
of other people who had seen him with
the child.
The crowd was orderly and \ after the
man had been hanged it quietly dispersed.
There were about 200 men engaged in the
affair and they were . all masked. \u25a0 They
attacked the jail door with a battering
ram and it soon yielded. .--
On gaining admittance they demanded
the keys of the jailer at the point. of a
gun and threatened If he did not yield
the man up they would kill him. * The
jailer then got the. man out of his cell
and he was given to the mob. When they
first took him Brady asked:
"What is it, gentlemen?"
HELENA, Mont., Oct. 2.-James Ed
ward Brady, the man who committed an
unusually brutal assault upon 5-year-old
Ida Pugsley in Helena yesterday, was this
morning, about 1:30 o'clock, taken/from
the jail by a mob and hanged to a tele
graph pole in the Haymarket square,
about three blocks from the jail. .

Samuel Isaacson Becomes Husband
of Mrs. Carrie Bryant to Better
Fulfill Promise.
Justice of the Peace Long officiated at a
sad ceremony last Monday when he joined
Samuel Isaacson ' in wedlock to, Mrs.
Carrie Bryant, who is lying on her death
bed. The wedding .took place at 203%
Third street and was witnessed by A. Mc-
Cullouch arid M. Pedderson.
Mrs. Bryant is 33 years of age and has
a little girl 7 years of age. She had often
requested Isaacson to take care of her
daughter after she died, and it was
tnought'that he would have a better legal
right to do so if he married the mother.
Isaacson is 21 years of age. The mother
and newly made wife is slowly succumb
ing to the ravages of the disease that has
laid her low. 7
Glover the nominee of the convention,
and the physician took the stage and in
dorsed the plattorm and made the usual
promises.
"\*7hen it was announced that nomina
tions for Police Judges were in order Mr.
Currie again cained .the recognition of
the chairman, and as he took the plat
form he was cheered for his courage.
Mr. Currie aroused merriment by an
nouncing that' he had another candidate
who desired to give $10 to the birds. He
named Edwin M. Sweeney for Police
Judge. The speaker said his candidate
was not on the. slate, and he therefore
asked the delegates to show their inde
pendence and vote for him.
Lon E. Prescott of the Fortieth District
followed, and in' a eulogistic speech he
named Charles A. Low. H. G. W. Dinkel
spiel swung into line and named Frank
P. Hayne. Norman. W. Hall, a young
Republican from the Thirty-ninth Dis
1 trict, named John W. Koch.- Merten Cal
len and Mr. Hillman seconded the nom
ination of Mr. Hayne.
The surprise of the -convention was the
appearance of "Fat Jack" O'Connor, who
mounted the platform, paid his 110 and
named John W. Koch. The delegates
warned O'Connor that hi-s candidate had
already been named and advised him to
take back his money. The alacrity he
displayed in getting his money convulsed
the delegates. â¢>
The roll was then called and the vote
announced was as follows:

Nominations of Supervisors.
Chairman Riordan then announced that
Frank P. Hayne and Charles A. Low were
bly been kidnaped. A white man and
Chinese belonging to the Bow Ong \u25a0 so
ciety called at the restaurant Monday
afternoon and the highbinder pointed to
HIng Yow, saying "that is the man." The
white man pulled a paper out of his
pocket and said It was a warrant for
HIng Yow's arrest. HIng Yow's friends
wanted to see the, warrant, but the man
refused to show It and hustled Hing Yow
out of the place.- Hing Yow's friends
have since been unable to find any trace
of him. . '
Mr. Greenbaum tried to bring order out
of chaos by asking for an adjournment
When the uproar was at its height Mr.
Hillman jumped Into the breach and
asked that the names be read before the
nominations closed. He said the dele
gates were desirous, of learning for whom
they were going to vote. He asked that
order be maintained and the delegates
listen to the names presented and weigh
them well before they voted.

. Edmund Tauzsky , tried to make a
speech, but his remarks were drowned in
an uproar of cheering and jeers. He suc
ceeded in. gaining attention by taking the
gavel. As temporary chairman he suc
ceeded in quieting the delegates. â¢
Myrtle Cerf tried ,to make a motion, but
had to desist. "Fat" Jack O'Connor
moved that nominations close.

the nominees of the convention.
Nominations for Supervisors were then
in order and a delegate moved that there
be no nominating speeches. The motion
prevailed. Myrtle Cerf of the Thirty
eighth then made a motion that the nomi
nations be made by districts. Marc
Anthony seconded the motion and the
chairman ruled It. out of order." Anthony
took an appeal from the chair and a deal
of â¢ discussion was had. Leon Samuels
closed the long \u25a0winded speeches by mov
ing, that the appeal be laid on . the table.
A roll-call resulted in 259 ayes and eighty
eight noes. The chair ruled that the ap
peal was laid on the table, and despite
the points of order and motions stated
that nominations for Supervisors were in
order. For a time the nominations could
not be heard. The delegates, therefore,
satisfied themselves by handing the secre
tary the names of the candidates.
Chairman Riordan called the conven
tion to order at 8:20. The first business
of the evening was the nomination of a
candidate for City and County Attorney.
Mai-k Sheldon took the platform and
named Joseph E. Barry for the office.
Charles Sonntag seconded the nomination
and there being none other named, the sec
retary was instructed to cast the ballot
of the convention. Barry was then
escorted to the platform and after the
cheering bad subsided expressed his ap
preciation of the honor conferred upon
him and said if elected he would conduct
the office in a business-like manner.
John R- Hillman had me pleasure of
nominating John Farnham for the office of
Public Administrator. He paid a high
tribute to his candidate. He said Mr.
Farnham was unable to visit the various
districts during the last election owing to
Illness and had he been able to make a
canvass he would undoubtedly have been
elected, as he only lost the office by a few
hundred votes. C. E. Murdock paid a
high tribute to Mr. Farnham. He said he
>>bH honesty, backbone and all the other
qualifications that were necessary to be
elected.
He took occasion to say that the Forty
first District delegates were In the con-
\ r ention as an object lesson; that they
were there In defense of a principle, and
although they had little opportunity to
help make up the ticket, they would take
their medicine like men and would return
to their district without sore heads or
hearts and hoped they would meet the
other delegates on a more auspicious oc
casion.
When the applause had passed the
nominations were close! and the ballot of
the convention was cast for the candidate.
Mr. Farnham then mounted the platform
and was applauded. He indorsed
the platform, and thanked the delegates
for nominating him.
\u25a0 The next business in order was the
nomination of a candidate for Tax Col
lector. J. S. Partridge named Edward J.
Smith in a flowery speech and Leon Sam
uels seconded the nomination. The sec
retary cast the ballot of the convention
and Mr. Smith took the platform and
promised to fulfill the duties of the office,
if elected, to the best of his ability and
to the satisfaction of the taxpayers.
Attempt to Break the Slate.
Merten C. Allen took the platform and
deposited another 510 piece for the privi
lege of nominating a "live man for a dead
office." He named Dr. C. A. Glover for
Coroner. The speech was clever and won
the applause of the delegates. W. W.
Shannon seconded the nomination.
The first attempt to crack the slate was
made at this time. Arthur Currie mount
ed the platform and named Dr. A. D. Mc-
Lean. His speech was greeted with long
cheers by the gallery, which was anxious
to see the oiled wheels of the convention
slip a cog. Mr. Currie remarked that the
Forty-first District \u25a0was the only one
.not in captivity. When the laughter had
died out he begged the delegates to show
their independence by voting against the
elate. He wanted to see a small crack
in it. he said.
When the applause had ceased Marc
Anthony seconded the nomination of Dr.
McLean. The vote resulted as follows:
Dr. Glover 275 2-3
Dr. McLean 70 1-3
Chairman Riordan then declared Dr.
The highest vote, 298, was secured by
George B_ Sanderson. The popularity of
Horace Wilson. Dr. Boxton and T. "W.
Collins was attested by a large vote.
The chairman of the convention last
night after adjournment announced the
following committtee on purity of elec
tion:
L J. Truman, P. H. Frendergast, M. L.
Asher, Edward Kelly and Frank J. French.
The foregoing committee, together with
Chairman Riordan, is authorized to act as
the campaign committee.
The customary three cheers for the
ticket were not called for when the-con
vention adjourned. The nominations are
not received with -enthusiasm. The
friends of Asa R. Wells predict his elec
tion, however, and several of them re
marked yesterday that his election to the
Mayoralty would put the San Francisco
delegation behind him for Governor next
year.

There was quite a dispute In the Thirty
eighth District about the selection of
county committeemen. The wrangle be
came so great that the chairman had
hard work keeping order. Martin Kelly,
to end the dispute, moved that the list
of county committeemen sent in by the
chairman of the Twenty-eighth be ac
cepted. Despite Delegate Currie's demand
for a roll call the motion was put and
carried.
A vote of thanks was extended to Chair
man Riordan and the secretaries.
The chairman* was empowered to ap
point a committee of twenty-five on puri
ty of election, and the county committee
was empowered to fill all vacancies on
the ticket caused by resignation or death.
Professor J. A. Rindge Is
Stricken With Heart
Failure. '
COMMITTEE IS DIVIDED.
Democrats Consider the Question of
Precedence in Nominations.
The committee on order of business of
the Democratic Municipal Convention met
yesterday afternoon in the office of Chair
man McGee to consider whether it was
advisable to name the Board of Super
visors before the nominee for Mayor was
selected. The committee was divided on
the question. Another meeting will be
held this afternoon to decide the matter.
The order in which municipal officers are
designated in the charter will be followed,
with possibly the exception of Mayor.
â¢The committee on platform and resolu
tions met during the afternoon and laid
out the lines that will be followed. It is
proposed in a general way to indorse the
present municipal administration.
Democrats Looking- for Mayor.
â¢ The Democratic locai convention will
meet at Odd Fellows' Hall this evening.
It is reported that a nomination for
Mayor will not be made to-night. The
leading candidates mentioned are Harry
Creswell. Dr. C. A. Leonard, Dr. Leland
and Supervisors Hotalingr and Branden
stein. Dr. Washington Dodge, who is on
a vacation in the country, has been asked
to lead the party in. the local campaign.
Professor Joseph A. Rindge, a venerable
teacher of ancient languages In a college
at Mobile. Ala., 'dropped dead last night
at the home of F. B. McElriach, 1014
Golden Gate avenue. "While walking
through the hallway he was stricken with
heart disease and before medical attend
ance- could be called he had passed away.
At the time of iiis death Professor.
Rindge was 80 years of age. He intended
visiting John T. Doyle, the well-known
lawyer, .who resides at Menlo Park, and
had made preparations to leave for the
home of his friend to-day.
, Professor Rindge was well known to
the residents of this city. For many
years he was engaged in teaching Greek
and Latin in Sacred Heart College. He'
received an offer from a big educational
institution in Mobile, Ala., some years
ago and accepted it. He was extremely
popular and a man of high attainments.
He came to this city a week ago to meet
his old pupils and friends and was being
warmly welcomed. The news of his de
mise will be a surprise to his scholars.

Growney's Death Saves Her.
The . case of Kittle Mahoney, charged
with grand larceny for stealing $140 from
Theodore Growney in a \u25a0 room at Eighth
and Folsom streets about ten days ago,
was dismissed by Judge Conlan yester
day. Growney died suddenly last Sunday.
